Only readers who are fans both of fantasy adventure stories and also of steamy romances will properly appreciate this series.  Those who are fans of just one of those two genres (whichever it is) may get frustrated by the amount of time and attention given to the other genre.<br /><br />As a romance novel, the sex scenes are much too detailed for my taste.  The author doesn't seem to have realized that less is more, and that such a scene can be far more compelling if there's at least something left to the reader's imagination.  This series (at least the first couple novels of it -- all I can comment on so far) doesn't allow any involvement by the reader's imagination.  Every slightest movement or sensation is described at great length in pornographically explicit detail.  Steamy, yes, and I guess some readers may like that, but for me it just makes that aspect of the book fall rather flat.<br /><br />I found the book far more compelling, however, as a fantasy adventure novel.  Even there, however, there were issues with it.  Some are minor enough to just come across as odd.  (For instance, in at least a couple different passages the author uses the word \\"translate\\" as though it were a synonym for \\"understand\\", which of course it isn't, and neither of those passages involved anything being translated.)  More significantly, there are numerous inconsistencies in the story:<br />* A series of events that occur early in the book are brought up later in the book as having been two years ago.  Yet later still, they're said to have occurred just three months ago.  (Based on all the events in between and the time they're said to havetaken, the \\"two years\\" version fits better than the \\"three months\\" version, yet the three month duration is significant to its portion of the story despite being inconsistent with the rest of the story.)<br />* The group makes a daring plan to deal with an enemy, but then changes part of that plan.  The strange thing is that they still go through with the most dangerous aspect of the original plan despite it serving no purpose in the new plan.<br />* A character gasps in amazement at the stunning revelation of something that she's known all along (and in fact was present when it happened).<br />* In a room full of vampires, one vampire accuses another of actions which, though illegal by human law, shouldn't be any issue by vampire law.  These accusations are quite unaccountably taken seriously, although no reason is given as to why they would be.<br />* The most troublesome inconsistency occurs in the climactic chapter.  Without getting into spoilers, I can only say that it involves a wager placed on the outcome of a contest.  The outcome of the contest is very clear, and the winner receives the prize agreed upon.  Yet just moments later, the loser of that wager claims rights to what he would have won had he won the wager, and accuses another of trying to steal it from him.  The remainder of the chapter is a series of back and forth one-upmanship regarding what types of claims have precedence over what other types of claims.  Yet at no point does anyone point out the obvious fact that the loser of that wager had no claim on its prize in the first place.<br /><br />With so many inconsistencies, it somewhat surprises me that I could enjoy this book as much as I did.  Yet the writing is vivid, the story is gripping, the characters are well-fleshed out, and throughout it kept me eager to read what happened next.  Objectively, I should probably knock off one star for the way the romance was handled and another for all the inconsistencies, and so give this book just a three-star rating.  But in the end \\"I like it\\" is more accurate than \\"It's OK\\", so I'm going to go ahead and boost my rating to four stars.  And now I'm going to go buy the next book in the series.

There are differences between the Iron Man I grew up and the one here, such as the appearance and origin of this episode's villain, Whiplash, the skin color of Nick Fury, or that in the old days Tony Stark kept his identity secret except for his inner circle (\\"Happy\\" Hogan, though not Pepper Potts, and including Don Blake/Thor). But if you're not a purist, is this pure escapist fun?<br />Maybe not pure -- it has Tony Stark dealing with some inner demons -- but for the most part, yes.<br /><br />If I had a choice between which to see again between this, the first Iron Man, and the Avengers, this would be an uncontested third place. However, if I go to a friends house and he puts this into the DVD, I'd sit back and enjoy.

B008JFUP8A|4.0|Although many people did not seem to like this 2013 movie, I thought it was decent.  OK, it was no award winner, but I enjoyed it and did not feel like I wasted my time (hour and a half approximately) or money.  It was obviously a Smith family film, with Will and son Jaden acting, Will and M. Night Shyamalan on the writing team, and Jada Pinkett Smith on the production team.  If I saw right, it was filmed in Costa Rica, Utah, and Southern California.<br /><br />The time period is long after planet Earth (hence, the title \\"After Earth\\") has become desolate, and long after the aliens who fed on humans have eradicated the human race on Earth.  We meet the Rangers, a military command from another planet.  Young Jaden is a Ranger Cadet, but seems to have some issues and just cannot achieve Ranger status.  I believe they are fear issues holding young Jaden back.<br /><br />I am guessing that Jaden is supposed to be around age 15 or so in the movie.  He has spent most of his life growing up without his dad, because dad was away on Ranger duty.  Jaden blames himself for his sister's death, so he is carrying heavy baggage.  When Will comes home, and before he retires, he takes Jaden off on a trip with him, to spend some guy-time together.  The plane hits an unexpected asteroid field and is forced to land on Earth, which is a dangerous place to be.  (All of this happens in the beginning of the film.)  The plane breaks in two pieces, about 100 kilometers apart and since Will is injured, Jaden must travel alone, but with Will guiding him from the ship.  The objective is to retireve a working distress beacon from the distant wreckage section and send the emergency signal for help.<br /><br />Although the first part of the movie is a bit slow, Jaden's journey is where it takes off.  I would love to say more, but you have to watch the movie now.  The CGI was pretty neat as they successfully blended it with holograms, bio-technology, scenery, and animatronics.  Although young Jaden's performance was not exceptional, I thought he did a good job. He played a young man who needed to listen, learn, and face his fears. I believe the movie captures that nicely.<br /><br />There are some great underlying messages in this storyline, the most penetrating being Will Smith saying to Jaden, \\"Danger is very real, but fear is a choice.\\"  Will goes on to explain in a simple paragraph that fear is an illusion which does not benefit us and we must choose how we respond to it.  He explains to Jaden that he must \\"live in the moment\\" and that fear is a projection of future events that might never even happen.  THESE ARE VERY WISE WORDS.  There are also parts of the movie that imply the son is reading his father's mind.  You will also see the father has taught his son to stop, ground, and center himself.  There is also an amazing scene where one of nature's creatures repays a debt to Jaden.  Let me also mention the underlying message that the earth has become desolate because we failed to take care of it.<br /><br />Nice movie. I do plan to buy the video when it comes out and watch it again.  I think the lesson about fear is priceless.  And although some reviews seem to want to bust Jaden out for not being as good as his father, I think the boy played his part well. When you watch the movie, I believe you will see that was part of the concept.  Instead of having a seasoned actor go out into the forbidden area, the boy character stretched himself to achieve above and beyond his fears and that added much to the story.  Actually, the more I think of the embedded messages in this movie, the more I like it.  Wouldn't it be great if we could all teach our children \\"Danger is very real, but fear is a choice.\\"<br /><br />6/9/13 - On a side note, yesterday, I found myself starting to worry about something and suddenly heard Will Smith's words to his son again.  He reminded me that fear is an illusion of the mind, where you are wasting time and energy getting upset over something that may never happen.  So I shifted my focus and went on about my business. . .mething that may never happen.  So I shifted my focus and went on about my business. . .

193700788X|1.0|*Spoilers*<br /><br />Having faithfully read this series since well before True Blood, I wanted to like this book.  True, the last few books were disappointing, but I hoped Charlaine Harris would end the series with a book I could rate five stars.  Unfortunately, I couldn't even come close.  I had to knock off one star for each of the following:<br /><br />Mystery Plot Holes<br /><br />The plots against Sookie just didn't make sense, never mind that they all happened at the same time.  Why would Copley sell his soul for a wish for a magical object to make a wish?  Why would he then want to ruin Sookie just for using that magical object she had no idea he wanted, especially after being warned off by the devil who has his soul?  Why would Glassport hate Sookie because she \\"nearly\\" ruined things for him (but didn't succeed) and didn't try to save him in Rhodes (but he survived anyway )?  Why would Claude need Glassport and Newlin to help him \\"track\\" Sookie; he knew exactly where she lived and worked?  And why wouldn't Niall, who had sent Claudine to protect Sookie and been following her via Eric for even longer, neglect to warn Sookie that Claude was back?  Saying that \\"Putting himself in someone else's shoes is not the Niall way\\" is insufficient explanation.  Also, since when can fairies be killed with regular bullets?  That would make them a much less formidable foe than they've been portrayed in past books and short stories.<br /><br />Although this doesn't relate to the mysteries, there is also the glaring hole of why Sookie thinks vampires will leave her alone after a year of Karin's protection.  Or why other supes (and even humans) would let her live a normal life.<br /><br />Pacing and Tone<br /><br />There were too many times when the story seemed to meander and drag.  The change in POV itself didn't bother me, but what did feel \\"off\\" was the coy use of \\"medium man,\\" \\"tall man,\\" \\"New Orleans businessman,\\" etc.  Why not just use their names - especially Copley'sand Tyrese's, as they were identified a few pages later?  And there were passages like, \\"I had that icky sensation of sweat trickling down between my butt cheeks\\" and \\"we slid together like seals\\" during sex, that were particularly cringe-inducing.  Of course, my biggest \\"Oh, how could she do this?\\" reaction was for letting Appius, a rapist and pedophile, win and sell Eric into sexual slavery.<br /><br />HEA<br /><br />Much of the fan disappointment with this book has centered around Sookie ending up with Sam, which is perfectly understandable given the *way* in which that was done.  The build-up to the romantic resolution, which has been such a fundamental part of most of the series, should not have to come about because of a magical device changing feelings; that's extremely sloppy plotting, especially if Harris has been planning this outcome for 12 years throughout 12 books.  And, even with the cluviel dor, Sam was only Sookie's *second* choice.  (\\"I guess that Eric read you better than I did.  He knew that *if he let me go*, you were standing first in line waiting.\\"  Doesn't that mean that, if Eric had *not* \\"let Sookie go,\\" she would have happily stayed with him?)  Sam was rendered a rebound.<br /><br />Moreover, this HEA required rewriting canon (although Sookie said she couldn't read Sam's mind here, in the short story that follows, \\"If I Had a Hammer,\\" she reads his thoughts clearly) and that Sookie expressly not care that her feelings for Sam might be caused by the cluviel dor (and that his definitely were), notwithstanding that she had spent *five books* worrying about whether the blood bond was influencing her feelings for Eric.  (It wasn't.)<br /><br />Perhaps most unbelievable to me is that, in order to get bail money from Eric, Sam would agree not to court her.  (Nor is it ever explained why Eric would impose such a condition, nor why it was so hard to come up with the money.)  Surely, if Sam were truly in love with Sookie, he would not agree to giveher up permanently in exchange for a little time without bail.  (Sookie's house could have been put up as collateral the next day.)<br /><br />Sookie<br /><br />My biggest disappointment is the change in Sookie herself.  In Dead Reckoning, Mr. Cataliades explained her \\"essential spark\\" as \\"an openness to the other world.\\"  Sookie lost that in this book.  She became intolerant (\\"Maybe there was more truth to Eric's accusations about my prejudice against vampire culture than I'd believed.  But vampires were users of humans\\").  Compare that to what Sookie told Sam in From Dead To Worse (\\"People aren't any better or worse than the supernaturals\\") or in All Together Dead (\\"two years ago . . . I didn't know that vampires were as different from each other as we are\\").  She also became self-absorbed; she had no sympathy for Jane in jail, finding it \\"disgusting\\" that \\"she was sick from both ends,\\" notwithstanding that she realized that \\"Jane was at heart a kind person\\" who wanted to help Sookie.  Compare this Sookie with the one who risked her life in Dead Until Dark to rescue Bill, the vampire she had just met, from drainers.  She even showed no appreciation for the fact that Eric agreed to an extra 100 years of slavery to keep her safe.  In this book she also suddenly - and repeatedly - swore.  Finally, although Sookie ends the book by saying that she would be fine alone, her actions showed her jumping into bed with Sam when her \\"previous lover had barely left town,\\" the day after hearing about Eric's sacrifice.  So, not only did Sookie not *grow* in this book, as I would have hoped at the end of a series, she actually regressed.<br /><br />What a disappointment!

0143122061|4.0|I thoroughly enjoyed this book.<br /><br />It sat on my shelf for about a year because I wasn't all that impressed with Fergusons's Ascent of Money, which I thought was both a little dry and a little too conservative. I thought this book was much better, but it still suffers from what I call Niall Ferguson's emphasis on conservatism-that is, he often resorts to saying history happened a certain way because it was more or less inevitable, whereas I see things as being more defined by chance and accident, history's grand themes needn't have turned out the way they did, in my opinion anyway.<br /><br />The best thing about this book is the very wide coverage of ideas. I have long wanted information on e.g. the French Revolution (why did it go so bad after such a useful basis at least?), what Marx was really up to and about, why China and the east in general lagged the West from the Middle Ages, why North and South America developed so differently after the arrival of Europeans in the 15th century, and continue to do so, what is the main reasons for Africa's under-development, why soviet communism eventually failed, and why China is now the fastest developing nation on earth, and what all this means for the future. There is a wide coverage of ideas here, some unconventional, some not, but excellent examples and idiosyncratic tales are a strong point of Ferguson, making it thoroughly entertaining at the very least.<br /><br />The ideas are way too many to outline here. So much so, I intent to read the book again, to take it all in. Here are some selected examples:<br /><br />-John Locke had a good understanding of how best to set up government and its associations.<br />-South American cultural heritage of a strong caste-working class and elite social structures hinder and preclude truly representative and democratic governments, particularly in the realm of property rights.<br />-The consumer society tends to work to the betterment of everybody.<br />-Western Medicine has hitched the ride with various forms of colonisation and development, leading to better living standards.<br />-The origins of social Darwinism and Nazism can be found in imperialism in Africa in the 19th century.<br />-The origins of the first world war may have had something to do with cultural norms developed and fostered under African imperialism<br />-Jeans and rock music are two of the things that under-mined soviet communism. 'Russians don't need this cacophonic noise'.<br />-cultures which facilitate trust in business and venture capitalism tend to do much better than those that don't, for a variety of reasons.<br />-the west's moral (protestant) work ethic was a good preparation for secular capitalism, and might be argued to be a natural progression (Weber).<br />-China and the east in general have a chronic tendency to look inward. Only two ports in China allowed some selected foreign ships in Adam Smith's day.<br />-For a variety of reasons, a variety of Chinese Christian religious cults led to the deaths of millions under various 'rebellions' in the 19th and 20th centuries. Today, however the growth of Christianity in China is profound.<br />-the Islamic tendency to denigrate scientific development has led it to consistently under-develop in comparison to the west. (thorny area this), particularly militarily.<br />-A fundamental difference between Christianity and Islam is the statement: 'render under Caesar the things that are Caesar's and render unto God the things that are Gods'. Ie the separation of church and state, deeply embedded in Christianity from the very beginning.<br />-Chinese issued grey pyjama-lookalikes under communism, and Islamic scarves and veils have something in common.<br />-there still isn't a free press in China, Russia, and Iran, for example.<br />-The French revolution's decline towards violation of it's founding principles and development of a military dictatorship was written into the very seeds of its' birth. Unlike the USrevolution, the French tended to elevate the general will above the law, they tended to implicitly trust authority, French society was also highly centralised and authoritarian, and the revolution itself originated in the bureaucracy, not the lower and middle classes. (They chose Rousseau instead of Locke, Ferguson puts it).<br /><br />One could go on and on. You get a whiff of Ferguson's overt conservatism in the above, but he argues and backs it up very well. It is a better book than one might think at first, and deals with many deep issues. Worth reading twice.revolution, the French tended to elevate the general will above the law, they tended to implicitly trust authority, French society was also highly centralised and authoritarian, and the revolution itself originated in the bureaucracy, not the lower and middle classes. 0553382152|5.0|I was given this book by a friend who asked me to read it and then talk with her about life after death.  After I read it, I felt more calm and certain about the existence of an afterlife.<br /><br />Shortly after reading it, the author was on Oprah Winfrey.  Unlike many other authors, she was not seeking celebrity nor validation, nor even monetary gain.  She was a very simply spoken woman who wrote about her near death experience and what she believes lies ahead for all of us.  From a life accounting of all the pain we have created for others to a realization of the joy we have also brought to others' lives, to a greater understanding of those 'deja vu' moments we all have, this book is an account of her experience of life after death.<br /><br />I have given a copy to many people who have lost loved ones (especially when those who have died were young and full of promise). B002QE3CTY|4.0|I am a total sucker for mazes. Always have been. The weirder and more fiendish, the better. Hedge mazes, cornfield mazes, video game mazes, paper and pencil mazes-- I just like the sense of being lost in an alien place.<br /><br />As such, the setting is what drew me to read The Maze Runner. A maze miles across, with no apparent exit, walls that shift every night, and bizarre monsters roaming its pathways, is instant sign-me-up territory. It didn't disappoint-- the world Thomas wakes into is an excellently strange and creepy one, and easily kept me up until 2 am.<br /><br />The action scenes were fast-paced and exciting. I'm not always a big fan of books relying heavily on action-- it can be tough to write well and keep interesting for very long-- but Dashner managed it. It helped, I think, that the Grievers were really disturbing. I don't say that about a lot of monsters in YA, but I didn't want to come face-to-face (well, okay, face-to-mass-of-shapeless-flesh-and-whirring-blades-- see? disturbing) with one of these critters any more than Thomas did.<br /><br />I had slightly mixed feelings about the actual plot. For most of the book it wasn't really clear what the everloving heck was going on. I was okay with that, because bits and pieces were presented in a tantalizing way that sort of fit with the feeling of being trapped in a maze-- a twisting, seemingly arbitrary place where it's very difficult to get a grip on the big picture. I enjoyed following along and wondering where it was all going.<br /><br />The ending, though, didn't bring the clarity I was hoping for. While it was sort of explained what was going on, it wasn't really clear that the explanation made any sense at all. Of course it's the start of a series, and there's plenty of time for things to become clear later on. But going solely by the contents of this book, I'm not convinced that the underlying reasons for everything are going to make any more sense no matter how much more they're explained. That said, I'm still hooked and will definitely read on to find out.<br /><br />I wasn't completely a fan of Dashner's writing style. He does action and setting very well, but when it comes to characterization his writing can get kind of flat and tends to tell rather than show. I didn't feel as connected to many of the characters as I'd have liked, and as such certain scenes didn't pack the punch that they might have. Most of the time that would be a dealbreaker for me; in this case, the other aspects of the book kept me turning the pages fast enough that I didn't really mind. But it was unfortunate-- I think stronger characterization could have turned this from a good story to an outstanding one.<br /><br />So, not perfect. But if you're looking for a fast-paced, intriguing read in an awesome setting, definitely give this a shot.

0596009208|5.0|As somebody with a history in C, C++, and PHP, Head First Java's unique format was an excellent change of pace for me. Using simple language but stressing the appropriate terms, the unique humurous format makes this my first programming book that I can simply read for an hour and leave with a good grasp of what was covered.<br /><br />I would not recommend the book for somebody that has no experience/limited experience with other languages, due to the lack of deep example code/follow-along style formatting. None of the characters seemed to behave with their usual personalities and drive.  I read the entire book, wanting to give it the benefit of the doubt, even though I almost stopped reading when Sookie was arrested for murder. Oh please, tell me this book gets better. But it really didn't.<br />  Most of the book left me saying \\"huh?\\" I do wonder if this is really Charlaine writing this at all??? There are outright contradictions in Sookie's abilities (suddenly she can't read Sam's thoughts?) But mostly it was the tone of all the characters, like pale one dimensional versions of their old selves. Eric just submits to the new arrangements, even agreeing to add an extra 100 years to the marriage. No fight, no revolt. I suppose if more books were forthcoming, he could get his Viking warrior mojo back and stage a coup, but this is how it's ending?!<br />  I have always suspected Sookie would end up with Sam in the end, so she can have children. At least all the readers knew it was a possibility. But there was no real bridge from long time good friends to suddenly passionate lovers. Use of the Cuviel Dor is the suggested bridge, but the interaction of Sookie and Sam after she saved him was just downright weird. So there was absolutely no build up of a shift from friend to lover.<br />  Then there was the appearance of Claude as one of the protagonists. That whole scenario was just weird and senseless. The other protagonist made no sense at all either, Amelia's father.<br />  I gave this book 2 stars rather than one, because I did enjoy a trip back into Sookie land, albeit a disappointing one. It was nice to see a reappearance of old friends come to help her in a time of need. Sookie wonders if anyone will help her, and she finds out she has many loyal friends after all.<br />  The vampires seem to make only cameo appearances in this book. None of the normally conniving, rascally, self serving antics we have come to know and love. Sookie's triple attraction of beauty, mind reading and part fairy blood should have had a mile of vamps and supers waiting in the wings to woo such a prize once she was divorced. After all she doesn't seem that upset about it. Maybe numb, but certainly not devastated. Even Bill, who professed undying love for Sookie, doesn't even make a stab at it.<br />  While I can see Pam taking over Fangtasia, I was nonplussed she automatically became sheriff too. What about Bill being Sheriff? Where's the vamp power struggle?<br />  After this book, I don't care anymore how Charlaine projects the future for her characters. I can't trust her to finish the life story of these characters true to their original presentations.<br />  Very sad and disappointing  way to end this series.

B004EWFV9K|5.0|In THE TROUBLED MAN,Henning Mankell brings to a close the fictional life and times of Swedish police officer Kurt Wallander. Throughout the series, Mankell has constructed an eminently believable account of an ongoing career of a policeman who seems to have been created fully formed as a career law enforcement officer who solves crimes while all the while battling personal demons. He drinks too much, eats irregularly, has a dysfunctional relation with his father, daughter, and wife, and refuses to take even the most elementary precautions to preserve his health. Yet in novel after, novel, Wallander gets results. The bad guy is always apprehended. Mankell's legions of faithful readers have grown to expect no less. But here, Mankell shows us a truly troubled man. Is this man of troubles a high-ranking Swedish naval officer who disappears and Wallander is tasked to find out why? Or is he Wallander himself who early on in the book shows increasing evidence of a creeping onset of dementia? Wallander is continually falling asleep during daytime, suffering mental blackouts, and forgetting who he is, where he is, and even why he is. This is, of course, most depressing for readers who might wonder why Mankell is so determined to portray a previously indestructible hero as yet one more victim of the cruel ravages of time. And cruel these ravages are. They weigh so heavily as a suffocating blanket of nihilism that one might be forgiven for thinking that Mankell's real purpose for this final entry was not to provide a satisfying exit for Kurt Wallander but rather to write a polemic disguised as fiction. Still, as I read through this long last look at Kurt Wallander, I was reminded of the Wallander of the previous books. THIS was the man whom I shall treasure as the essence of Mankell's vision. The Kurt Wallander of the final chapter was a bitter reminder that no one may safely and eternally pursue his dreams of a better society without the possibility of failing to uncover all the subtle clues of one's own fate.the subtle clues of one's own fate.

193700788X|1.0|I cannot express how glad I am that this series that I once loved is now finished. I'm not sure why the series had to decline so sharply in quality over the last few books, but it did. And with this last book, it continued that decline. No, it didn't continue that decline; it jumped off a cliff and sank to the very bottom of the ocean in quality. It wasn't just a little bad. It was horrible. It was awful. It was a cross between mind-numbingly bad and just plain torturous.<br /><br />I know that some have been quick to say that the backlash related to this book has to do with who people 'shipped Sookie with. Even though I'm a Sooric (Sookie/Eric shipper), I can honestly say that that has nothing to do with my distaste for this book. And I'm a bit perturbed that people think that upset fans are really that shallow. I would have been fine with her ending up with just about anyone in the franchise, as long as there was a proper build-up to the relationship. And even if it ended with a sudden relationship, like it did, I wouldn't have dismissed the book as being bad just for that.<br /><br />No, the reasons that this book sucks are a bit more complex than that.<br /><br />1.) For the first nine books, Sookie had a definitive voice. She was likable. She was funny. She wasn't always the brightest or least-self involved character in the world, but she wasn't the most shallow and she wasn't horribly rude. At some point in the tenth book, this changed. Sookie became more hateful. She became more judgmental. She became obsessed with the most idiotic things. She became a character that disgusted me. By the time I finished this book, I felt like I had been reading from the perspective of the villain for the last 13 books. She was just rude, bigoted, and selfish. She tried to make it seem like she was a good person, but it was quite clear in her attitude and dismissal of certain characters (and their species' in general) that she was just as bad, if not worse, than the antagonists. Oh, and she wasn't just judgmental of bad guys. No, she even judged Tara for still being two sizes bigger than she was pre-pregnancy. She just had twins and Sookie is judging her for not bouncing back quick enough. Pathetic. Absolutely pathetic. Oh, and everything that Eric does in this book is somehow wrong. He comes to Sookie's house to tell her that he still cares for her and is leaving someone outside to protect her since he can't, and she gets pissed. He bails her out of jail, she gets pissed. He wants to keep her in his (un)life, she gets pissed. He agrees to being away from Pam and Karin for a longer period of time to keep her protected, she gets pissed. He makes it so that she can't be hurt by any vampire ever, she gets pissed. She is just so pissy in this book.<br /><br />2.) For a character who has been sexually assaulted and a writer who has as well, the lack of compassion for Eric being sold into sex slavery with his marriage to Freyda was appalling. (His being forced to have sex with Freyda is described as a cushy benefit of his job.) What was also appalling is that even after Bill attempted to rape Sookie in Club Dead and after finding out that their whole relationship was built on him using her, meaning that any sex that she consented to was consented to under false pretenses and was, therefore, akin to rape, Sookie is still attracted to him. She describes his kissing like it's magical and wonderful. She lets him in her house whenever he wants. She doesn't care that he brings along a character in the beginning of the book that is actually out to possibly impune her character. She gets all googly eyed when he's around. If she can so easily dismiss Eric for being a vampire and therefore soulless, evil, etc., then why can't she do that with Bill? Why is he immune to her bigotry?<br /><br />3.) At one point, Sookie says that she doesn't think that her relationship with Eric was meant to be because he wanted to save it using the cluviel dor. She doesn'tthink magic should be used to save or create a relationship. She then turns around and hooks up with her \\"HEA\\" because the has opened his eyes to the fact that he wants to have a relationship (with wild seal sex) with her. If magic can doom her relationship with Eric, why can't it doom the relationship with Sam? Because, technically, the only reason for their hookup is that she used magic.<br /><br />4.) For twelve books, Sookie was the sole narrator. For twelve mysteries, we only knew what was happening in the story based on what Sookie knew. For twelve books, that format was good enough for all involved. Why, in the thirteenth and final book, do we have to go from first person (Sookie) to third person? It was annoying. It made it harder to read. And it just seemed lazy.<br /><br />5.) Speaking of laziness, there were times when it seemed like CH just half-assed her way through chapters and scenes. Either we were being told every single mundane thing that some random character was doing, or we would get one or two sentences that seemed more like they were part of an outline for the story. It was annoying and I can't believe that someone along the way didn't pick up on how bad that made it look. Of course, there were other editing issues with this book, so I'm wondering if maybe the editor just ignored all the issues that were going on in the book.<br /><br />6.) When Tyrese is given HIV as part of a Faustian deal (on page 17), I got a bit pissed. Using an illness as a punishment for someone selling their soul is just wrong on so many levels. It makes it seem like it is okay to say that anyone who ends up with a disease, especially a deadly disease, is somehow responsible for ending up with that disease. It makes it seem like diseases are punishments from a supernatural force. Equating the cause for having a disability with selling your soul/sinful behavior? It is offensive. Absolutely offensive.<br /><br />7.) The character interactions were off. The whole story lacked flow, but the character interactions were some of the worst. It was almost like watching a student film where no one in the film actually knows how to act. Basically, reading the interactions was like watching a trainwreck...in slow motion.<br /><br />8.) Speaking of the characters, was it really necessary to have so many of the less-important characters from past books in this particular book. Did we really need Barry and Quinn and Mr. C and Diantha and Amelia and Bob and Copley and Johan and on and on and on? I know that Charlaine probably wanted to have Sookie have this one last battle with all her friends and enemies, but it wasn't really necessary. Sometimes less is actually more, especially when it comes to ending a series. It just seemed like overkill.<br /><br />I didn't enjoy this book. I can't imagine anyone who has been a fan of the series enjoying it. I can't even imagine non-fans enjoying it. If there were a way to give it an even more negative rating, I would because it deserves it. This book was nothing but a waste of time. It makes the whole series, a series that I used to enjoy, seem like a gigantic waste of time. Avoid this book if you can. Even if--no especially if--you loved the series.think magic should be used to save or create a relationship. B00B52F278|3.0|Spartacus has been somewhat of a tumultuous ride throughout the show's history.  It began on the wrong foot, showing off generic action against bad special effects while characters spoke cheese.  A certain John Hannah carried us through the show until it hit its stride and there became one of the best shows on television for a season and a half until we faced the inevitable: Andy Whitfield was dead and not coming back, as was the character Hannah played.  Suddenly the show relied on a new actor in the role of the show's namesake to take up arms against a villain in Glaber that proved incompetent at the best of times.  They tried, bless their hearts, but the show struggled and lost direction.  Roman soldiers suffered Storm Trooper syndrome, being proven so weak compared to the escaped gladiators that any ten were not worth even a single of the weakest gladiators while Spartacus' character devolved into slow motion screaming and bouts of random speech-giving while intricately-planned battles between equal opponents and character development were put on the backburner.<br /><br />So here we come to Spartacus: War of the Damned, the final season of this epic ride, and almost immediately it became the tale of two shows; the good and the bad.  The good is the villains of the piece.  Crassus is easily Spartacus' most formidable foe yet, and gives a nuanced portrayal that at times can make you love or hate the character in a similar fashion to Hannah's Batiatus.  Much the same with Caesar, whose young arrogance is quickly proven to be well-deserved in a surprising way.  Meanwhile Crassus' son Tiberius provides someone the audience can properly despise.  This is a good set of villains most stories would beg to have as characters who can drive the plot forward and give the heroes of the story something realistically formidable to face off against.  So what's the problem?<br /><br />The problem is the rebellion side of the show.  The former slaves.  The problem begins immediately from the very first scene.  See, it's been months since Vengeance left off.  Spartacus' army has grown to the tens of thousands, if not more, and they trounce around Roman territory defeating big Roman armies.  What's the problem with this, you might ask?  Vengeance, you see, was very favorable toward our protagonists.  All the goals they had set upon joining the rebellion or escaping into freedom were met.  Spartacus got his revenge, Crixus got Naevia back, Gannicus redeemed himself to Oenomaus and everyone else, the couple HUNDRED or so who followed Spartacus, was safely free as they had wanted.  So why are they now an enormous army randomly smashing occasional Roman armies?  What is their new goal, or motivation?  This is never once answered until only a couple episodes are left and then they only name those goals to tell use that they've changed.  Just as bad, the characters are more like caricatures, a bunch of people just going through the motions of what the audience expects while occasionally given a slow motion jumping/screaming sequence.  It's bloody, it's violent, it's sexy, it's boring.  Midway through the season they decide to focus an episode on the Roman camp and it wasn't until midway through that I realized most the rebels had received all of three of the fifty minutes given the episode, and that I did not miss them at all.<br /><br />Such is how it goes for most of the season, as the Romans carry the show while rebels go through the motions and we're left cheering on classic Spartacus and his stubborn invincibility, or classic Gannicus and his drunken partying, or classic Crixus and his passion, without much real development happening.  This begins changing in the final few episodes, where they begin focus on furthering the development of certain individuals, but it is too often abridged (as in the case of Crixus, who is given months of glorious battle as he strikes out on his own...told in the space of under ten minutes) or worse (as in the case of Gannicus and Sibyl) it is completely forced.  All the while we struggle figuring out what these characters actually WANT, what they desire the end result to be.  On the flipside, the Roman camp becomes a mess of tangled plotlines, largely thanks to Crassus' personal slave Kore whose storyline slips into far too many others, pulling them together and going a whole lot of nowhere.  By the end I'd call the episodes some of the worse in the series since the original pilot but for a fantastic finale which tied everything together in almost the best it possibly could given everything which came before.<br /><br />What then to make of Spartacus: War of the Damned?  It is certainly enjoyable.  If you loved the characters before then you'll more than likely keep loving them and want to see them to the bitter end.  It has plenty of enjoyable action and sex scenes to grab your attention.  The Romans are a pure joy to watch and I would not mind at all tuning into a spinoff series focused around Caesar, Crassus and the like.  BUT, the season suffers from a lot of major storytelling issues and is poorly focused upon developing too many new characters and plots and is not the season-long sendoff one might hope it would be.

0307291146|5.0|Anyone who reads learns quickly not to judge a book by its cover. And in this case it is doubly important because neither the look nor the title serve as any indication of what is inside this classic tome. It could be argued that it is impossible to \\"master the art...\\" by reading a book, and I would agree. What is important, however, is realizing the breadth of understanding that can be gained by a careful reading of this work. It is an important work not only because of its timeliness when it was in its infancy and first published, but because it shares a timeless look into the history of the culinary arts as seen through eyes shaded by French gastronomy.<br /><br />I watched the movie \\"Julie & Julia\\", the theme from which was derived from the over five hundred recipes in this two volume set, and I also remember some of the episodes from Julia Childs early television career. I truly enjoyed the movie, but the early Julia Child television stuff was not something that modern television producers would consider a first class effort. I think, perhaps, that we become somewhat jaded by modern television and the almost universal accessibility of convenience based communication.<br /><br />Thankfully, bound printed journals such as this do not suffer the decay that is so prevalent in today's digital world. You can be proud of the wonderful books that you own. You can read and re-read them and enjoy them for many years, then pass them down to others who are free to enjoy them too. \\"Mastering the Art of French Cooking\\" belongs to that very select group that I own...and will never part with. This set joins many others, but is one of a few that I really enjoy re-reading. Every time I take them from their place on the shelf and page through them, I learn something new. Gaining knowledge is in itself a good thing.<br /><br />No, you will not become a master French chef by reading these volumes. You will, however, gain a terrific insight into French gastronomy and pick up some great tips. All of which will serve you well as you strive to prepare that special dish or try to understand the reasoning behind \\"mother sauces\\" and how it affects the homemade mac & cheese dish that your child relishes.<br /><br />There are many reasons to read and own \\"Mastering the Art of French Cooking\\", but staying current with modern French cuisine is not one of them. If however, you love 'continental' cuisine or maybe just have an insatiable desire to better understand the history of gastronomy that pre-dates modern canned and packaged goodness, then this two volume set will serve you well. And, who knows, you too may be enticed to travel the road of Julie and prepare all five hundred plus recipes firmly embedded in \\"Mastering the Art of French Cooking\\" and in the history of food world wide.<br /><br />Enjoy!eat tips. B00AFEXRME|1.0|A routine commercial flight from Orlando to Atlanta goes dreadfully wrong when a flight-control malfunction occurs.  The aircraft's pilot, a former naval aviator hopped up on booze and drugs, does what should've been impossible - recover an MD-80 series aircraft from an uncontrollable dive and with his first officer manage to land it in a field, near a church, and right across a stream from where baptisms are occurring.  Alas, one of the flight attendants dies, which means the NTSB must investigate, putting the pilot's career in jeopardy and leading to a major crisis in courage.<br /><br />In real life, an Alaska Airline MD-83 aircraft, a relative of the MD-80, did experience such a failure, putting the aircraft into an extreme dive, in early 2000.  Though the pilots were able to recover from one dive, they couldn't recover from a second, even when they tried to roll the aircraft inverted.  Eighty-three passengers and five crew members died that day.  Which means that when the MD-80 in \\"Flight\\" tried the same thing, it should've all been over.<br /><br />\\"Flight\\", then, is a movie built around a premise that it simply cannot sustain.  In fact, everything after the crash is essentially a moot point.  All this despite normally reliable star power than includes John Goodman (who was grating), Don Cheadle (who was wimpy), and Denzel Washington (who did not have Rosario Dawson, Chris Pine, and 2,000 tons of locomotive to bail him out as he did in \\"Unstoppable\\").<br /><br />A much better movie in a similar vein is \\"The High and The Mighty\\", which is considered to be one of the prototypes of the modern disaster movie. There are wonderful mixing choices here, and always a total unwillingness to compromise his artistic vision. B005GG0MIS|5.0|Nancy Gibbs and Michael Duffy have accomplished a true tour de force with \\"The Presidents Club.\\"  One cannot read the book without coming away with a profound reevaluation of presidents from Truman to Obama.  Many pay lip service to how difficult is the job of the president, but never have those pressures and crises been explored through the eyes of the members of this most exclusive club.  Our country is more and more divided along ideological lines with compromise becoming a mortal sin.  But regardless of one's political ideology, this book offers an in depth and apolitical analysis that transforms our often two-dimensional memory of past presidents into three-dimensional people who cared deeply and struggled against extraordinary odds about doing what they saw as best for the country.<br /><br />One may violently disagree with a certain president's domestic or foreign policy, but after reading this book, no one should fail to recognize how much the president struggled with seemingly intractable issues.  At a time of divisiveness and ridicule, \\"The Presidents Club\\" challenges us to stop demonizing those with whom we disagree and rather understand how and why decisions were made in the most lonely of jobs.  We see in fascinating detail how all of them believed in making our country a better place.  We also are shown the extent to which they put aside politics to offer support and encouragement to the current office holder.<br /><br />Quite simply, a book that should change the way we think about our leaders.

B00BQ1D7X4|5.0|Alice in Chains has done it again. Not only have they made a fantastic album that is well over an hour of music and chock full of great guitar work, melody, tempo shifts, and two part vocal harmonies, but it is RARE in this day and age where the industry is not what it once was and rock music as a whole has taken a backseat in the mainstream for an album like this to ever see the light of day.<br /><br />Because of this it's quite an event for me to sit back and take in everything TDPDH has to offer; especially from a band that for all intents and purposes should've died along with the tragic death of Layne Staley. Yet here we are, and here AiC is as strong as ever with what has to be one of their best releases to date. I felt compelled to give my track by track take on the album:<br /><br />  Hollow - This song is 5 months old for me and despite that it still stands up as one of the best on the album. It's an interesting leadoff track as they don't usually open with something as moody and dark as this song is, but it works. The lyrics are very poetic (also dark) and when the second part of the chorus kicks in at the midway point it really takes the entire song to another level (which is also a sneak preview to how several of the songs on this album are structured). Great guitar solo by Jerry on this one (the first of several).<br /><br />  Pretty Done - The first time I heard the grungy power riff that starts this song off I instantly thought of Tripod. Then the sheen of Duvall's sleekly produced vocal comes towering in on the verse which is also very reminiscent of how they made Layne sound on that album (Again comes to mind specifically) and it confirms that this song could've been lifted right off that album. Lots of tempo shifting throughout after the second chorus and it's hard to pinpoint a conventional song structure (the first of several on the album). Really dig this song and love how it closes out with the extra vocal bit and slightly faster tempo at theend.<br /><br />  Stone - The other well known song that's now been with me for over two months, and it also still stands up as a strong addition to the album with its intense and just wicked pulverizing riff. Like the song structure as well on this one as there isn't really a conventional chorus but just the tempo shifts that happen in the verses themselves. The soaring guitar solo from Jerry is one of the best in their entire catalog.<br /><br />  Voices - STRAIGHT OFF JAR OF FLIES. The amazing and atmospheric intro/outro of this song is one of those detail oriented things that take a song from good to great (or great to amazing if you're talking about this song as far as I'm concerned). The verses are very pop sounding in the delivery of the two part vocals but the power of the vocals behind the simple acoustic riff really sets the tone of the verses (and the song itself). It continues as the chorus comes swooping in out of nowhere and grabs your attention every time it appears. The urgent sounding melodic riffs that periodically appear just simply puts the icing on the cake. This is the type of song this band has always done extraordinarily well, and this one is definitely no exception. Not only a favorite but probably a new entry into my top 10 AiC songs ever.<br /><br />  The Devil Put Dinosaurs Here - I was skeptical about this song going in as I've never been the biggest fan of the title, but my skepticism quickly disappeared as soon as I heard it. The metallic sounding main riff is one of the most hauntingly ominous riffs I've ever heard. I really like how the vocal delivery in the verses are structured and are equally as haunting and just as ominous as the main riff itself. The rest of the guitar work and solo are also just top notch. Very original and effect laden song (the small riff that could pass as a dinosaur is a nice touch) that doesn't let up or get stale at all during its 6 and a half minutes. Another song that features a great outro as well.<br/><br />  Lab Monkey - This song starts off at a very slow pace and continues down that path for the first 2 minutes.  Just when my interest starts to wane the song shifts into something completely different with a very melodic groove and then after an even higher tempo change slows back down to some interesting sounding breakdown. Definitely one of the more interesting AiC songs and it took me a few listens to really `get' it, but once I did it's up there for me with all of the rest.<br /><br />  Low Ceiling - `Old Mr. Fun is back...wonder where he's been hiding at?' Love the melodic intro to this song with the classic Layne style (AHHHH) vocal over the top (that also gets repeated again before the solo). One of the more upbeat songs on the album relying on a simple melodic progression in the verse and then vocal rich chorus. Guitar solo would sound right at home on an Aerosmith or Slash album. Great song, and yet another one that progresses into a great outro.<br /><br />  Breath on a Window - HUGE highlight for me. Another upbeat song that starts off with your vintage Jerry riff and chorus that wouldn't sound out of place on one of his solo albums. What comes just after the 2 minute mark is where the song gets put into a completely different stratosphere. This was briefly previewed on the Itunes sample and cuts off JUST as this part was starting, and I thought to myself then that the song sounded like it was going somewhere really cool. I wasn't wrong. The melodic riff that kicks in here is great and continues for a bit, and then goes into some riff that reminds me of Whale & Wasp off JOF. Then it shifts into this FANTASTIC melodic outro that I won't even try to describe because I simply wouldn't do it justice. Quickly has become one of my favorite ever AiC songs.<br /><br />  Scalpel - Acoustic number that is almost bordering on being country-esque. When the electric guitar comes bellowing in over the acoustic it really gives it a nice touch. Bridge is a nice segueway into the great two part vocal harmony in the chorus. The tempo shift in the middle before the final chorus gives the song some unpredictability which is obviously a strong point of this album as whole. Another song that wouldn't sound out of place on a Jerry solo record.<br /><br />  Phantom Limb - Not sure what's better in this song, the earth shattering tempo shifting smash your teeth in guitar riff or the amazing vocal delivery by Duvall. They go hand in hand remarkably well as the song takes you on a journey telling a story of what sounds like a desolate sounding wasteland. Then it all comes crashing down for the chorus before it blows back up again with a phenomenal guitar solo. This is probably my favorite song on the album (which is saying something considering how great this album is).<br /><br />  Hung on a Hook - ABSOLUTE VINTAGE AiC, and another song that could've been pulled right off Tripod. Slow melodic intro is great and the Layne style `AHHHHH' throughout the song is an amazing touch. The two part chorus really lifts off the song after the soft vocal delivery from Duvall in the verses and makes it another favorite. The guitar solo/closure is rather unique and sends the song off nicely.<br /><br />  Choke - The acoustic/electric guitar trade off in the intro is awesome, and sets up the moody sounding verse nicely. The two part harmonies throughout the chorus again really make the song what it is. Another awesome guitar solo and overall great  and fitting sendoff to the album.<br /><br />My one criticism of the album is that there is again too much Jerry featured and not enough Duvall. I don't think it's a coincidence that the best songs on the two albums now that they've released with this lineup are songs that Duvall has fronted and/or co-written (Last of My Kind, Phantom Limb, Hung on A Hook).<br /><br />There isn't a single song on the album that I wouldn't classify as great so it's also tough to pick favorites (although if I had to PhantomLimb, Voices, Breath on a Window and TDPDH have probably been played the most so far).<br />Just an amazing album...THANK YOU AiC.

B002N1AEN2|2.0|I stumbled across XX's opening track (Intro) a few months back and was impressed enough to check out Infinity, which was also fairly decent.  I made a mental note to revisit the band, and when I recently needed to increase an Amazon order to qualify for free shipping, I took this as a sign that it was time to dive in.  Little did I know how shallow the water was.<br /><br />How this album got elevated to such high stature eludes me.  I get the mood music angle, and if the intent is to provide aural atmosphere slightly more interesting than a white noise generator, then The XX has clearly achieved their goal.  But all those 5 star reviews and top ten lists indicating that this is some type of musical masterwork?  I'm sorry, but this album was meant to be heard, not listened to.  Should you try to actually listen to it  and it's no trivial task staying focused on most of the tracks  the experience isn't worth the effort.<br /><br />The album opens with Intro, a great track that is almost out of place on this album.  Intro then gives way to VCR, arguably the worst song on this and maybe 100 other randomly selected albums, featuring the lamest lyrics I've heard in years: I wanna find myself by the sea, in another's company......  C'mon, seriously? I'm trying to imagine the musical context into which those cringe-worthy lyrics could reasonably be placed and, with the exception of an album entitled something like Sing along with Barney & Friends, I'm drawing a blank.<br /><br />Crystalised comes closer to being almost memorable, as well as using VCR's sea metaphor to less embarrassing though equally ineffective results.  I kind of liked the dramatic pause in Islands, and how it transitioned to a simple drum and guitar wrap up.  That is until I realized the pause was just the gap between Islands and Heart Skipped a Beat.  They sound like they could be the same song (and may have originally been the same for all I know) , but when the silence between tracks is mistaken for artistic effect?  That's not good.<br /><br />Fantasy isn't worth commenting on, but Shelter does deserve a serious listen, though not from this CD.  Google Birdy's cover of Shelter and listen to that.  Listening to Romy Madley Croft sing Shelter gives the impression of a bored millennial lying on her couch watching Dr. Who reruns and phoning in the vocal track during the commercials.  Birdy (all of 15 years old when she recorded her cover) delivers Shelter with more soul and raw emotion than is contained in the entirety of this CD.  And that's the problem here: the music is lifeless and the emotions seem detached at best and forced at worst.  When Romy and Oliver Sim sing at each other, we get romance that's more tepid than torrid, and heartbreak expressed on the same level of disappointment felt when one discovers the paper towel dispenser is empty.  As another reviewer mentioned here, The XX is just Xanax without the middle three letters.  The remaining tracks (except Infinity as previously mentioned) don't add anything worth noting.<br /><br />XX is not a bad CD, it's just not good.  Okay yes, VCR is bad - laughably bad in fact - but I can't trash an entire album over one track.  Even Radiohead's iconic OK Computer had Fitter Happier, and though I don't think The XX's intent with VCR was comparable, in both cases the albums would be improved by removing those tracks.  But the similarity ends there.  Radiohead insists that we listen, while The XX is clearly suggesting that we not bother.  So I'll take their suggestion and hit the eject button.

B000008JUM|4.0|On its face using the songs of Cole Porter to raise awareness of HIV/AIDS and money for combating it by using contemporary artists seemed a bit of a stretch.  Certainly his songs are very much in the cannon of \\"those in the know\\" and Broadway babies, but the question was how translatable to modernity these songs could be.  \\"Red Hot & Blue\\" answers that question to widely varying degrees and it quickly becomes an answer to the question about High Risk/High Reward for the artists involved.  Clearly some of the artists have nothing to lose by playing fast and loose and taking chances with Porter's songs and the payoff is quite often spectacular.  Right out of the gate Nenah Cherry serves up a chilling yet scintillating version of \\"I've Got You Under My Skin\\" the summons up the horrors of HIV/AIDS at the time.  Her take on this classic is spot-on-the-money and I wouldn't change a note as she truly gets the material.  The shift from Nenah Cherry to the smooth R&B of the Neville Brothers is a bit jarring, but Aaron Neville's gorgeous falsetto over the restrained yet lively arrangement is truly a beauty to behold. Sinead O'Connor serves up a decided retro version of \\"You Do Something to Me\\" that is a refreshing take from her other output at the time and showcases how lovely her voice is given the right material.  Roland Gift likewise shows what a great vocalist he is with \\"Love For Sale\\", a similarly nice restrained but lively take on that well known track with his band at the time (Fine Young Cannibals).  The odd sequencing really pops here as the smooth styling of Fine Young Cannibals bumps up against what is probably my absolute favorite track from \\"Red Hot & Blue\\" \\"Well Did You Evah\\" pairing up Debbie Harry and Iggy Pop.  Whoever cooked up this idea is a bloody genius as the two play off each other masterfully in a raunchy playful manner that is thoroughly modern and sounds like they had a blast in the studio.  If only the rest of \\"Red Hot & Blue had this much life, vitality, and energy!  From here the rest of the tracks slowly start to peter out as a bit lifeless an perfunctory.  David Byrne's \\"Don't Fence Me In\\" is a bit intriguing and fun and Annie Lennox's mournful take on \\"Ev'ry Time We Say Goodbye\\" is a profoundly moving tear jerker.  While U2's \\"Night and Day\\" was a single I still find this a bit of a snooze despite his menacing vocals.  K.D. Lang's \\"So In Love\\" is another profoundly moving take on Porter that really showcases how great a vocalist she is...I truly wish she would do an album of songs like this!  From here things go a bit downhill as the big groups/artists of the era take a stab at Porter with no real effort.  The Thompson Twins, Erasure, Jimmy Sommerville, and Aztec Camera all make appearances but their versions are so slight, sluggish, and perfunctory that they all come and pass with scarcely a moment's notice.  Lisa Stansfield is the sole standout in this section and as with K. D. Lang she REALLY should do an album of pop standards as her voice is ideally suited for this.  All in all \\"Red Hot & Blue\\" is a bit like a Whitman's sampler...it's designed to appeal to a broad section of people but not everything is going to delightful or to everyone's taste.tality, and energy! Lisa Stansfield is the sole standout in this section and as with K. D. Lang she REALLY should do an album of pop standards as her voice is ideally suited for this.  All in all \\"Red Hot & Blue\\" is a bit like a Whitman's sampler...it's designed to appeal to a broad section of people but not everything is going to delightful or to everyone's taste.

B00AWJYJVA|5.0|What a pleasure to read a book written by a master of his craft. Where others pad out their text with endless repetition; he is brevity itself. Others use tricks for generating suspense; he weaves a story so spellbinding that you literally cannot put the book (or Kindle) down. Others regurgitate endless, detailed research verbatim without attempting to bridge to the tenuous story line; he has deep and intimate knowledge of his subject, the British foreign service, but does not condescend or patronise us, his reader, but converses as with an equal, a fellow insider. Others struggle to describe people, places, situations resorting to video like transcriptions; his light, deft touch is illuminating, amusing and delightfully informative. Others paste disconnected story lines together through artificial coincidences; he weaves a plot so dense, so credible, so involving, which builds as the tale progresses, culminating in a crescendo at which point he deliberately pulls the rug out from under our feet in true, inimitable le Carre Spy from the Cold style leaving us wondering what happened next to him or to her, innocent bystanders inextricably woven into a web of intrigue, where decency breeds downfall; deceit success; governments collusion, dissemination, murder; and good old England and her values under siege from within and without due to greed, fear and control. Where others have you checking page numbers regularly to determine how much longer you must endure the torture; he has you cleft between wishing it would go on for ever and being released from the unendurable suspense.<br /><br />John le Carre has penned some if the greatest spy books ever written. This is one of his best.

B004FLKV5O|5.0|If I were to choose two complete &#34;Ring Cycles,&#34; I'd pick Bernard Haitink's as a studio recording, and Barenboim's as a live recording. Both are incredible. Barenboim's is recorded live at Bayreuth, but without an audience, so there isn't a lot of coughing, etc., and even the stage noise is minimal. The acoustics at Bayreuth will leave you breathless. The orchestra is glorious throughout, and if there's a complaint it doesn't have to do with the orchestra, but the way the singers were miked: In &#34;Die Walkure,&#34; every time a singer turns away from what must be a directional mike, the voice fades and the orchestra appears too loud. This problem is addressed in the other operas.<br /><br />What can I say about this &#34;Ring's&#34; singing? That the singing is so good that it's classic in many roles. John Tomlinson makes a terrific Wotan, Graham Clark as both Loge and as Mime is simply awesome. His voice reflects the characters' thoughts exactly. Poul Elming suffers only from the directional mike issue mentioned earlier, but apart from that his Siegmund is wonderfully beautiful, not a yell at all. Nadine Secunde as Sieglinde is also marvelously expressive, and well paired with Elming. Anne Evans as Brunhilde is at her first entrance a bit too light in tone: It sounds as though she came onstage without warming up. But this passes, and what is wonderful - and unique - about her Brunhilde throughout the whole cycle is her lighter, more lyrical singing of the role than I've ever heard. It's really wonderful. And all of Brunhilde sounds easy for her, full of expression.<br /><br />This recording is also the best I've heard Siegfried Jerusalem sing, as an awe-inspiringly beautiful Siegfried. He's still fresh in voice, extremely expressive, and the rigors of &#34;Siegfried&#34; don't affect him at all. It sounds easy for him. Same in &#34;Gotterdammerung.&#34; The recording is worth it just for that singing alone. I venture to say that, much to my shock, Jerusalem surpasses even Lauritz Melchior in the role, no mean feat!<br /><br />In all, this recording deserves its 5 stars. And I'm gladdened to have a &#34;modern&#34; recording of the Ring that surpasses the old, classic recordings of Melchior and Flagstad, for example. It is a wonderful experience.m surpasses even Lauritz Melchior in the role, no mean feat!<br /><br />In all, this recording deserves its 5 stars.
